class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n<div class="mw-indicator" id="mw-indicator-good-star"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Good_articles" title="This is a good article. Follow the link for more information."><img alt="This is a good article. Follow the link for more information." data-file-height="185" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Symbol_support_vote.svg/19px-Symbol_support_vote.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Symbol_support_vote.svg/29px-Symbol_support_vote.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Symbol_support_vote.svg/39px-Symbol_support_vote.svg.png 2x" width="19"/></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Outer space</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_space&amp;redirect=no" title="Interstellar space">Interstellar space</a>)</span></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Void between celestial bodies</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Outer_space_(disambiguation)" title="Outer space (disambiguation)">Outer space (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth%27s_atmosphere.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="420" decoding="async" height="500" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Earth%27s_atmosphere.svg/300px-Earth%27s_atmosphere.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Earth%27s_atmosphere.svg/450px-Earth%27s_atmosphere.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Earth%27s_atmosphere.svg/600px-Earth%27s_atmosphere.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earth%27s_atmosphere.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The interface between the Earth\'s surface and outer space. The <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n_line" title="K&#225;rm&#225;n line">K&#225;rm&#225;n line</a> at an altitude of 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi) is shown. The layers of <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">the atmosphere</a> are drawn to scale, whereas objects within them, such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>, are not.</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Outer space</b>, or just <b>space</b>, is the expanse that exists beyond the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and outside of any <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">astronomical object</a>. Outer space is not completely empty&#8212;it is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_vacuum" title="Hard vacuum">hard vacuum</a> containing a low density of particles, predominantly a <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_neutrino_background" title="Cosmic neutrino background">neutrinos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>. The baseline <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>, as set by the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_background_radiation" title="Cosmic background radiation">background radiation</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, is 2.7 kelvins (&#8722;270.45&#160;&#176;C; &#8722;454.81&#160;&#176;F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBE2008_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-CBE2008-1">[1]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Warm%E2%80%93hot_intergalactic_medium" title="Warm&#8211;hot intergalactic medium">plasma between galaxies</a> accounts for about half of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baryonic_matter" title="Baryonic matter">baryonic (ordinary) matter</a> in the universe; it has a <a href="/wiki/Number_density" title="Number density">number density</a> of less than one <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a> per <a href="/wiki/Cubic_metre" title="Cubic metre">cubic metre</a> and a temperature of millions of kelvins;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baas41_908_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-baas41_908-2">[2]</a></sup> local concentrations of this plasma have condensed into <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a>. Studies indicate that 90% of the mass in most galaxies is in an unknown form, called <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>, which interacts with other matter through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitational</a> but not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">electromagnetic forces</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreedmanKaufmann2005573,_599&#8211;601_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreedmanKaufmann2005573,_599&#8211;601-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trimble_1987_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trimble_1987-4">[4]</a></sup> Observations suggest that the majority of the <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass&#8211;energy equivalence">mass-energy</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a> is a poorly understood <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_energy" title="Vacuum energy">vacuum energy</a> of space, which astronomers label <i><a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_darkenergy_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_darkenergy-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreedmanKaufmann2005650&#8211;653_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreedmanKaufmann2005650&#8211;653-6">[6]</a></sup> Intergalactic space takes up most of the volume of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>, but even galaxies and <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">star systems</a> consist almost entirely of empty space.\n</p><p>Outer space does not begin at a definite altitude above the Earth\'s surface. However, the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n_line" title="K&#225;rm&#225;n line">K&#225;rm&#225;n line</a>, an altitude of 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi) above sea level,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO\'Leary200984_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO\'Leary200984-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space_begin_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-space_begin-8">[8]</a></sup> is conventionally used as the start of outer space in space treaties and for aerospace records keeping. The framework for international <a href="/wiki/Space_law" title="Space law">space law</a> was established by the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Space_Treaty" title="Outer Space Treaty">Outer Space Treaty</a>, which entered into force on 10 October 1967. This treaty precludes any claims of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_sovereignty" title="National sovereignty">national sovereignty</a> and permits all states to freely <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">explore outer space</a>. Despite the drafting of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_resolution" title="UN resolution">UN resolutions</a> for the peaceful uses of outer space, <a href="/wiki/Anti-satellite_weapon" title="Anti-satellite weapon">anti-satellite weapons</a> have been tested in Earth orbit.\n</p><p>Humans began the physical exploration of space during the 20th century with the advent of high-altitude <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balloon_(aircraft)" title="Balloon (aircraft)">balloon flights</a>, followed by manned <a href="/wiki/Rocket_launch" title="Rocket launch">rocket launches</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_orbit" title="Earth orbit">Earth orbit</a> was first achieved by <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a> of the Soviet Union in 1961, and <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_spacecraft" title="Unmanned spacecraft">unmanned spacecraft</a> have since reached all of the known <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. Due to the high cost of getting into space, manned <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">spaceflight</a> has been limited to low Earth orbit and the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.\n</p><p>Outer space represents a challenging environment for human exploration because of the hazards of vacuum and <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microgravity" title="Microgravity">Microgravity</a> also has a negative effect on human <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a> that causes both <a href="/wiki/Muscle_atrophy" title="Muscle atrophy">muscle atrophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight_osteopenia" title="Spaceflight osteopenia">bone loss</a>. In addition to these health and environmental issues, the economic cost of putting objects, including humans, into space is very high.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In 350 BCE, Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> suggested that <i>nature abhors a vacuum</i>, a principle that became known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Horror_vacui_(physics)" title="Horror vacui (physics)">horror vacui</a></i>. This concept built upon a 5th-century BCE <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontological</a> argument by the Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a>, who denied the possible existence of a void in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrant198110_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrant198110-9">[9]</a></sup> Based on this idea that a vacuum could not exist, in the <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">West</a> it was widely held for many centuries that space could not be empty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorterParkDaston200627_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorterParkDaston200627-10">[10]</a></sup> As late as the 17th century, the French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="Ren&#233; Descartes">Ren&#233; Descartes</a> argued that the entirety of space must be filled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckert20065_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckert20065-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">ancient China</a>, the 2nd-century astronomer <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Heng" title="Zhang Heng">Zhang Heng</a> became convinced that space must be infinite, extending well beyond the mechanism that supported the Sun and the stars. The surviving books of the Hs&#252;an Yeh school said that the heavens were boundless, "empty and void of substance". Likewise, the "sun, moon, and the company of stars float in the empty space, moving or standing still".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedhamRonan198582&#8211;87_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedhamRonan198582&#8211;87-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Italian scientist <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> knew that air had mass and so was subject to gravity. In 1640, he demonstrated that an established force resisted the formation of a vacuum. However, it would remain for his pupil <a href="/wiki/Evangelista_Torricelli" title="Evangelista Torricelli">Evangelista Torricelli</a> to create an apparatus that would produce a partial vacuum in 1643. This experiment resulted in the first mercury <a href="/wiki/Barometer" title="Barometer">barometer</a> and created a scientific sensation in Europe. The French mathematician <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">Blaise Pascal</a> reasoned that if the column of mercury was supported by air, then the column ought to be shorter at higher altitude where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_pressure" title="Air pressure">air pressure</a> is lower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoltonBrush2001267&#8211;268_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoltonBrush2001267&#8211;268-13">[13]</a></sup> In 1648, his brother-in-law, Florin P&#233;rier, repeated the experiment on the <a href="/wiki/Puy_de_D%C3%B4me" title="Puy de D&#244;me">Puy de D&#244;me</a> mountain in central France and found that the column was shorter by three inches. This decrease in pressure was further demonstrated by carrying a half-full balloon up a mountain and watching it gradually expand, then contract upon descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECajori191764&#8211;66_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECajori191764&#8211;66-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magedurger_Halbkugeln_Luftpumpe_Deutsches_Museum.jpg"><img alt="A glass display case holds a mechanical device with a lever arm, plus two metal hemispheres attached to draw ropes" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3488" data-file-width="2616" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Magedurger_Halbkugeln_Luftpumpe_Deutsches_Museum.jpg/170px-Magedurger_Halbkugeln_Luftpumpe_Deutsches_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Magedurger_Halbkugeln_Luftpumpe_Deutsches_Museum.jpg/255px-Magedurger_Halbkugeln_Luftpumpe_Deutsches_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Magedurger_Halbkugeln_Luftpumpe_Deutsches_Museum.jpg/340px-Magedurger_Halbkugeln_Luftpumpe_Deutsches_Museum.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magedurger_Halbkugeln_Luftpumpe_Deutsches_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The original <a href="/wiki/Magdeburg_hemispheres" title="Magdeburg hemispheres">Magdeburg hemispheres</a> (lower left) used to demonstrate Otto von Guericke\'s vacuum pump (right)</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1650, German scientist <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Guericke" title="Otto von Guericke">Otto von Guericke</a> constructed the first vacuum pump: a device that would further refute the principle of <i>horror vacui</i>. He correctly noted that the atmosphere of the Earth surrounds the planet like a shell, with the <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> gradually declining with altitude. He concluded that there must be a vacuum between the Earth and the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGenz2001127&#8211;128_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGenz2001127&#8211;128-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Back in the 15th century, German theologian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Cusanus" title="Nicolaus Cusanus">Nicolaus Cusanus</a> speculated that the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a> lacked a center and a circumference. He believed that the Universe, while not infinite, could not be held as finite as it lacked any bounds within which it could be contained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETassoulTassoul200422_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETassoulTassoul200422-16">[16]</a></sup> These ideas led to speculations as to the infinite dimension of space by the Italian philosopher <a href="/wiki/Giordano_Bruno" title="Giordano Bruno">Giordano Bruno</a> in the 16th century. He extended the Copernican <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliocentric" title="Heliocentric">heliocentric</a> <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a> to the concept of an infinite Universe filled with a substance he called <a href="/wiki/Aether_(classical_element)" title="Aether (classical element)">aether</a>, which did not resist the motion of heavenly bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGatti200299&#8211;104_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGatti200299&#8211;104-17">[17]</a></sup> English philosopher <a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">William Gilbert</a> arrived at a similar conclusion, arguing that the stars are visible to us only because they are surrounded by a thin aether or a void.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly196597&#8211;107_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly196597&#8211;107-18">[18]</a></sup> This concept of an aether originated with <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greek</a> philosophers, including Aristotle, who conceived of it as the medium through which the heavenly bodies move.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlenickApostolGoodstein1986356_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlenickApostolGoodstein1986356-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The concept of a Universe filled with a <a href="/wiki/Luminiferous_aether" title="Luminiferous aether">luminiferous aether</a> retained support among some scientists until the early 20th century. This form of aether was viewed as the medium through which light could propagate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHariharan20032_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHariharan20032-20">[20]</a></sup> In 1887, the <a href="/wiki/Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment" title="Michelson&#8211;Morley experiment">Michelson&#8211;Morley experiment</a> tried to detect the Earth\'s motion through this medium by looking for changes in the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> depending on the direction of the planet\'s motion. However, the <a href="/wiki/Null_result" title="Null result">null result</a> indicated something was wrong with the concept. The idea of the luminiferous aether was then abandoned. It was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>\'s theory of <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a>, which holds that the speed of light in a vacuum is a fixed constant, independent of the observer\'s motion or <a href="/wiki/Frame_of_reference" title="Frame of reference">frame of reference</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlenickApostolGoodstein1986357&#8211;365_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlenickApostolGoodstein1986357&#8211;365-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThagard1992206&#8211;209_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThagard1992206&#8211;209-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first professional <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomer</a> to support the concept of an infinite Universe was the Englishman <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Digges" title="Thomas Digges">Thomas Digges</a> in 1576.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaor1991195_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaor1991195-23">[23]</a></sup> But the scale of the Universe remained unknown until the first successful measurement of the distance to a nearby star in 1838 by the German astronomer <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Bessel" title="Friedrich Bessel">Friedrich Bessel</a>. He showed that the star <a href="/wiki/61_Cygni" title="61 Cygni">61 Cygni</a> had a <a href="/wiki/Stellar_parallax" title="Stellar parallax">parallax</a> of just 0.31&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond">arcseconds</a> (compared to the modern value of 0.287&#8243;). This corresponds to a distance of over 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_year" title="Light year">light years</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWebb199971&#8211;73_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWebb199971&#8211;73-24">[24]</a></sup> In 1917, <a href="/wiki/Heber_Doust_Curtis" title="Heber Doust Curtis">Heber Curtis</a> noted that <a href="/wiki/Nova" title="Nova">novae</a> in spiral nebulae were, on average, 10 magnitudes fainter than galactic novae, suggesting that the former are 100 times further away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curtis1988_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curtis1988-25">[25]</a></sup> The distance to the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a> was determined in 1923 by American astronomer <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a> by measuring the brightness of <a href="/wiki/Cepheid_variable" title="Cepheid variable">cepheid variables</a> in that galaxy, a new technique discovered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henrietta_Leavitt" title="Henrietta Leavitt">Henrietta Leavitt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csiro_20041025_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-csiro_20041025-26">[26]</a></sup> This established that the Andromeda galaxy, and by extension all galaxies, lay well outside the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETysonGoldsmith2004114&#8211;115_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETysonGoldsmith2004114&#8211;115-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The modern concept of outer space is based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_Bang_cosmology" title="Big Bang cosmology">"Big Bang" cosmology</a>, first proposed in 1931 by the Belgian physicist <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lema&#238;tre">Georges Lema&#238;tre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature127_3210_706_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature127_3210_706-28">[28]</a></sup> This theory holds that the universe originated from a very dense form that has since undergone continuous <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">expansion</a>.\n</p><p>The earliest known estimate of the temperature of outer space was by the Swiss physicist <a href="/wiki/Charles_%C3%89douard_Guillaume" title="Charles &#201;douard Guillaume">Charles &#201;. Guillaume</a> in 1896. Using the estimated radiation of the background stars, he concluded that space must be heated to a temperature of 5&#8211;6&#160;K. British physicist <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Eddington" title="Arthur Eddington">Arthur Eddington</a> made a similar calculation to derive a temperature of 3.18&#160;K in 1926. German physicist <a href="/wiki/Erich_Regener" title="Erich Regener">Erich Regener</a> used the total measured energy of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a> to estimate an intergalactic temperature of 2.8&#160;K in 1933.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apeiron2_3_79_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Apeiron2_3_79-29">[29]</a></sup> American physicists <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ralph_Alpher" title="Ralph Alpher">Ralph Alpher</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Herman" title="Robert Herman">Robert Herman</a> predicted 5&#160;K for the temperature of space in 1948, based on the gradual decrease in background energy following the then-new <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apeiron2_3_79_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Apeiron2_3_79-29">[29]</a></sup> The modern measurement of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> is about 2.7K.\n</p><p>The term <i>outward space</i> was used in 1842 by the English poet Lady <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emmeline_Stuart-Wortley" title="Emmeline Stuart-Wortley">Emmeline Stuart-Wortley</a> in her poem "The Maiden of Moscow".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStuart_Wortley1841410_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStuart_Wortley1841410-30">[30]</a></sup> The expression <i>outer space</i> was used as an astronomical term by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt" title="Alexander von Humboldt">Alexander von Humboldt</a> in 1845.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Humboldt184539_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Humboldt184539-31">[31]</a></sup> It was later popularized in the writings of <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a> in 1901.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-entymonline_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-entymonline-32">[32]</a></sup> The shorter term <i>space</i> is older, first used to mean the region beyond Earth\'s sky in <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">John Milton</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost">Paradise Lost</a></i> in 1667.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harper2001_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-harper2001-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_state">Formation and state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Formation and state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1980" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg/310px-CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg/465px-CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg/620px-CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This is an artist\'s concept of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">metric expansion of space</a>, where a volume of the Universe is represented at each time interval by the circular sections. At left is depicted the rapid <a href="/wiki/Inflationary_epoch" title="Inflationary epoch">inflation</a> from the initial state, followed thereafter by steady expansion to the present day, shown at right.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a></div>\n<p>According to the Big Bang theory, the very early Universe was an extremely hot and dense state about <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">13.8&#160;billion years ago</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planck_2013_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-planck_2013-34">[34]</a></sup> which rapidly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">expanded</a>. About 380,000 years later the Universe had cooled sufficiently to allow protons and electrons to combine and form hydrogen&#8212;the so-called <a href="/wiki/Recombination_(cosmology)" title="Recombination (cosmology)">recombination epoch</a>. When this happened, matter and energy became decoupled, allowing photons to travel freely through the continually expanding space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciAm301_1_36_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-SciAm301_1_36-35">[35]</a></sup> Matter that remained following the initial expansion has since undergone gravitational collapse to create <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">astronomical objects</a>, leaving behind a deep vacuum that forms what is now called outer space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESilk2000105&#8211;308_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESilk2000105&#8211;308-36">[36]</a></sup> As light has a finite velocity, this theory also constrains the size of the directly observable universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciAm301_1_36_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-SciAm301_1_36-35">[35]</a></sup> This leaves open the question as to whether the Universe is finite or infinite.\n</p><p>The present day <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">shape of the universe</a> has been determined from measurements of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">cosmic microwave background</a> using satellites like the <a href="/wiki/Wilkinson_Microwave_Anisotropy_Probe" title="Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe">Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe</a>. These observations indicate that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spatial_geometry" title="Spatial geometry">spatial geometry</a> of the observable universe is "<a href="/wiki/Flatness_(cosmology)" title="Flatness (cosmology)">flat</a>", meaning that photons on parallel paths at one point remain parallel as they travel through space to the limit of the observable universe, except for local gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMAP_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-WMAP-37">[37]</a></sup> The flat Universe, combined with the measured mass density of the Universe and the accelerating <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">expansion of the Universe</a>, indicates that space has a non-zero <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_energy" title="Vacuum energy">vacuum energy</a>, which is called <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESparkeGallagher2007329&#8211;330_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESparkeGallagher2007329&#8211;330-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Estimates put the average energy density of the present day Universe at the equivalent of 5.9 protons per cubic meter, including dark energy, <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baryonic_matter" title="Baryonic matter">baryonic matter</a> (ordinary matter composed of atoms). The atoms account for only 4.6% of the total energy density, or a density of one proton per four cubic meters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_wmap_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_wmap-39">[39]</a></sup> The density of the Universe, however, is clearly not uniform; it ranges from relatively high density in galaxies&#8212;including very high density in structures within galaxies, such as planets, stars, and <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a>&#8212;to conditions in vast <a href="/wiki/Void_(astronomy)" title="Void (astronomy)">voids</a> that have much lower density, at least in terms of visible matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aj89_1461_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-aj89_1461-40">[40]</a></sup> Unlike matter and dark matter, dark energy seems not to be concentrated in galaxies: although dark energy may account for a majority of the mass-energy in the Universe, dark energy\'s influence is 5 <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">orders of magnitude</a> smaller than the influence of gravity from matter and dark matter within the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rvmphys_75_559_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-rvmphys_75_559-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_Ultra_Deep_Field_part_d.jpg"><img alt="A black background with luminous shapes of various sizes scattered randomly about. They typically have white, red or blue hues." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hubble_Ultra_Deep_Field_part_d.jpg/290px-Hubble_Ultra_Deep_Field_part_d.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hubble_Ultra_Deep_Field_part_d.jpg/435px-Hubble_Ultra_Deep_Field_part_d.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hubble_Ultra_Deep_Field_part_d.jpg/580px-Hubble_Ultra_Deep_Field_part_d.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_Ultra_Deep_Field_part_d.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Part of the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Ultra-Deep_Field" title="Hubble Ultra-Deep Field">Hubble Ultra-Deep Field</a> image showing a typical section of space containing galaxies interspersed by deep vacuum. Given the finite <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>, this view covers the past <span class="nowrap">13&#160;billion years</span> of the <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_universe" title="Chronology of the universe">history</a> of outer space.</div></div></div>\n<p>Outer space is the closest known approximation to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfect_vacuum" title="Perfect vacuum">perfect vacuum</a>. It has effectively no <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a>, allowing stars, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planets" title="Planets">planets</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moons" title="Moons">moons</a> to move freely along their ideal <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbits</a>, following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_formation" title="Planetary formation">initial formation</a> stage. However, even the deep vacuum of <a href="/wiki/Outer_space#Intergalactic" title="Outer space">intergalactic space</a> is not devoid of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a>, as it contains a few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atoms" title="Hydrogen atoms">hydrogen atoms</a> per cubic meter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pasj20_230_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-pasj20_230-42">[42]</a></sup> By comparison, the air humans breathe contains about 10<sup>25</sup> molecules per cubic meter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBorowitzBeiser1971_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBorowitzBeiser1971-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-patrick_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-patrick-44">[44]</a></sup> The low density of matter in outer space means that <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> can travel great distances without being scattered: the <a href="/wiki/Mean_free_path" title="Mean free path">mean free path</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a> in intergalactic space is about 10<sup>23</sup>&#160;km, or 10&#160;billion light years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies197793_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies197793-45">[45]</a></sup> In spite of this, <a href="/wiki/Extinction_(astronomy)" title="Extinction (astronomy)">extinction</a>, which is the <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)">absorption</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scattering" title="Scattering">scattering</a> of photons by dust and gas, is an important factor in galactic and intergalactic <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fitzpatrick2004_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-fitzpatrick2004-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Stars, planets, and moons retain their <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmospheres</a> by gravitational attraction. Atmospheres have no clearly delineated upper boundary: the density of atmospheric gas gradually decreases with distance from the object until it becomes indistinguishable from outer space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChamberlain19782_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChamberlain19782-47">[47]</a></sup> The Earth\'s atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> drops to about <span class="nowrap">0.032 </span><a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">Pa</a> at 100 kilometres (62 miles) of altitude,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-squire2000_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-squire2000-48">[48]</a></sup> compared to 100,000 Pa for the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> (IUPAC) definition of <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">standard pressure</a>. Above this altitude, isotropic gas pressure rapidly becomes insignificant when compared to <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_pressure" title="Dynamic pressure">dynamic pressure</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Thermosphere" title="Thermosphere">thermosphere</a> in this range has large gradients of pressure, temperature and composition, and varies greatly due to <a href="/wiki/Space_weather" title="Space weather">space weather</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jmsj_85B_193_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-jmsj_85B_193-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The temperature of outer space is measured in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_theory_of_gases" title="Kinetic theory of gases">kinetic</a> activity of the gas, as it is on Earth. However, the radiation of outer space has a different temperature than the kinetic temperature of the gas, meaning that the gas and radiation are not in <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">thermodynamic equilibrium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrialnik2000195&#8211;196_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrialnik2000195&#8211;196-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitzer197828&#8211;30_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitzer197828&#8211;30-51">[51]</a></sup> All of the observable universe is filled with photons that were created during the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, which is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">cosmic microwave background radiation</a> (CMB). (There is quite likely a correspondingly large number of <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_neutrino_background" title="Cosmic neutrino background">cosmic neutrino background</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fp2_30_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-fp2_30-52">[52]</a></sup>) The current <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">black body</a> temperature of the background radiation is about 3&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a> (&#8722;270&#160;<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">&#176;C</a>; &#8722;454&#160;<a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">&#176;F</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj707_2_916_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj707_2_916-53">[53]</a></sup> The gas temperatures in outer space are always at least the temperature of the CMB but can be much higher. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">corona</a> of the Sun reaches temperatures over 1.2&#8211;2.6&#160;million&#160;K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj325_442_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj325_442-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Magnetic fields have been detected in the space around just about every class of celestial object. Star formation in spiral galaxies can generate small-scale <a href="/wiki/Dynamo" title="Dynamo">dynamos</a>, creating turbulent magnetic field strengths of around 5&#8211;10&#160;&#956;<a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">G</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Davis%E2%80%93Greenstein_effect" title="Davis&#8211;Greenstein effect">Davis&#8211;Greenstein effect</a> causes elongated <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust grains</a> to align themselves with a galaxy\'s magnetic field, resulting in weak optical <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a>. This has been used to show ordered magnetic fields exist in several nearby galaxies. <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamics" title="Magnetohydrodynamics">Magneto-hydrodynamic</a> processes in <a href="/wiki/Active_galactic_nucleus" title="Active galactic nucleus">active</a> <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_galaxy" title="Elliptical galaxy">elliptical galaxies</a> produce their characteristic <a href="/wiki/Astrophysical_jet" title="Astrophysical jet">jets</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_lobe" title="Radio lobe">radio lobes</a>. Non-thermal <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_radio_source" title="Astronomical radio source">radio sources</a> have been detected even among the most distant, <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">high-z</a> sources, indicating the presence of magnetic fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WielebinskiBeck2010_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-WielebinskiBeck2010-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Outside a protective atmosphere and magnetic field, there are few obstacles to the passage through space of energetic <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particles</a> known as cosmic rays. These particles have energies ranging from about 10<sup>6</sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a> up to an extreme 10<sup>20</sup>&#160;eV of <a href="/wiki/Ultra-high-energy_cosmic_ray" title="Ultra-high-energy cosmic ray">ultra-high-energy cosmic rays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rmp83_3_907_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-rmp83_3_907-56">[56]</a></sup> The peak flux of cosmic rays occurs at energies of about 10<sup>9</sup>&#160;eV, with approximately 87% protons, 12% helium nuclei and 1% heavier nuclei. In the high energy range, the flux of <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> is only about 1% of that of protons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELang1999462_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELang1999462-57">[57]</a></sup> Cosmic rays can damage electronic components and pose a <a href="/wiki/Health_threat_from_cosmic_rays" title="Health threat from cosmic rays">health threat</a> to space travelers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide199311&amp;#45;217_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide199311&amp;#45;217-58">[58]</a></sup> According to astronauts, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Don_Pettit" title="Don Pettit">Don Pettit</a>, space has a burned/metallic odor that clings to their suits and equipment, similar to the scent of an <a href="/wiki/Arc_welding" title="Arc welding">arc welding</a> torch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ls2012_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-ls2012-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopSicSmell_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopSicSmell-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite the harsh environment, several life forms have been found that can withstand extreme space conditions for extended periods. Species of lichen carried on the ESA <a href="/wiki/BIOPAN" title="BIOPAN">BIOPAN</a> facility survived exposure for ten days in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Astrobiology_11_4_281_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Astrobiology_11_4_281-61">[61]</a></sup> Seeds of <i><a href="/wiki/Arabidopsis_thaliana" title="Arabidopsis thaliana">Arabidopsis thaliana</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nicotiana_tabacum" title="Nicotiana tabacum">Nicotiana tabacum</a></i> germinated after being exposed to space for 1.5 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Astrobiology_12_5_517_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Astrobiology_12_5_517-62">[62]</a></sup> A strain of <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_subtilis" title="Bacillus subtilis">bacillus subtilis</a></i> has survived 559 days when exposed to low-Earth orbit or a simulated martian environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Astrobiology_12_5_498_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Astrobiology_12_5_498-63">[63]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Panspermia" title="Panspermia">lithopanspermia</a> hypothesis suggests that rocks ejected into outer space from life-harboring planets may successfully transport life forms to another habitable world. A conjecture is that just such a scenario occurred early in the history of the Solar System, with potentially <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganism</a>-bearing rocks being exchanged between Venus, Earth, and Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholson2010_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholson2010-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_human_bodies">Effect on human bodies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Effect on human bodies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_exposure" title="Space exposure">Space exposure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weightlessness" title="Weightlessness">Weightlessness</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg"><img alt="The lower half shows a blue planet with patchy white clouds. The upper half has a man in a white spacesuit and maneuvering unit against a black background." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg/170px-Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg/255px-Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg/340px-Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Because of the hazards of a vacuum, astronauts must wear a pressurized <a href="/wiki/Space_suit" title="Space suit">space suit</a> while off-Earth and outside their spacecraft.</div></div></div>\n<p>Even at relatively low altitudes in the Earth\'s atmosphere, conditions are hostile to the human body. The altitude where atmospheric pressure matches the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure_of_water" title="Vapor pressure of water">vapor pressure of water</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Human_body_temperature" title="Human body temperature">temperature of the human body</a> is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armstrong_line" title="Armstrong line">Armstrong line</a>, named after American physician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harry_G._Armstrong" title="Harry G. Armstrong">Harry G. Armstrong</a>. It is located at an altitude of around 19.14&#160;km (11.89&#160;mi). At or above the Armstrong line, fluids in the throat and lungs boil away. More specifically, exposed bodily liquids such as saliva, tears, and liquids in the lungs boil away. Hence, at this altitude, human survival requires a pressure suit, or a pressurized capsule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPiantadosi2003188&#8211;189_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPiantadosi2003188&#8211;189-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Once in space, sudden exposure of unprotected humans to very low <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">pressure</a>, such as during a rapid decompression, can cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_barotrauma" title="Pulmonary barotrauma">pulmonary barotrauma</a>&#8212;a rupture of the lungs, due to the large pressure differential between inside and outside the chest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ajeas2_4_573_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-ajeas2_4_573-66">[66]</a></sup> Even if the subject\'s airway is fully open, the flow of air through the windpipe may be too slow to prevent the rupture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krebs_pilmanis1996_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-krebs_pilmanis1996-67">[67]</a></sup> Rapid decompression can rupture eardrums and sinuses, bruising and blood seep can occur in soft tissues, and shock can cause an increase in oxygen consumption that leads to <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">hypoxia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ajeas2_4_573_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-ajeas2_4_573-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As a consequence of rapid decompression, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> dissolved in the blood empties into the lungs to try to equalize the <a href="/wiki/Partial_pressure" title="Partial pressure">partial pressure</a> gradient. Once the deoxygenated blood arrives at the brain, humans lose consciousness after a few seconds and die of hypoxia within minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bmj286_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-bmj286-68">[68]</a></sup> Blood and other body fluids boil when the pressure drops below 6.3&#160;kPa, and this condition is called <a href="/wiki/Ebullism" title="Ebullism">ebullism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jramc157_1_85_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-jramc157_1_85-69">[69]</a></sup> The steam may bloat the body to twice its normal size and slow circulation, but tissues are elastic and porous enough to prevent rupture. Ebullism is slowed by the pressure containment of blood vessels, so some blood remains liquid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBillings19731&#8211;34_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBillings19731&#8211;34-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landis20070807_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-landis20070807-71">[71]</a></sup> Swelling and ebullism can be reduced by containment in a <a href="/wiki/Pressure_suit" title="Pressure suit">pressure suit</a>. The Crew Altitude Protection Suit (CAPS), a fitted elastic garment designed in the 1960s for astronauts, prevents ebullism at pressures as low as 2 kPa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-am39_376_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-am39_376-72">[72]</a></sup> Supplemental oxygen is needed at 8&#160;km (5.0&#160;mi) to provide enough oxygen for breathing and to prevent water loss, while above 20&#160;km (12&#160;mi) pressure suits are essential to prevent ebullism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllery200068_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllery200068-73">[73]</a></sup> Most space suits use around 30&#8211;39&#160;kPa of pure oxygen, about the same as on the Earth\'s surface. This pressure is high enough to prevent ebullism, but evaporation of nitrogen dissolved in the blood could still cause <a href="/wiki/Decompression_sickness" title="Decompression sickness">decompression sickness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Air_embolism" title="Air embolism">gas embolisms</a> if not managed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavisJohnsonStepanek2008270&#8211;271_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavisJohnsonStepanek2008270&#8211;271-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Human_evolution" title="Human evolution">Humans evolved</a> for life in Earth <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravity</a>, and exposure to weightlessness has been shown to have deleterious effects on human health. Initially, more than 50% of astronauts experience <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_motion_sickness" title="Space motion sickness">space motion sickness</a>. This can cause <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertigo_(medical)" title="Vertigo (medical)">vertigo</a>, headaches, <a href="/wiki/Lethargy" title="Lethargy">lethargy</a>, and overall malaise. The duration of space sickness varies, but it typically lasts for 1&#8211;3 days, after which the body adjusts to the new environment. Longer-term exposure to weightlessness results in <a href="/wiki/Muscle_atrophy" title="Muscle atrophy">muscle atrophy</a> and deterioration of the <a href="/wiki/Human_skeleton" title="Human skeleton">skeleton</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight_osteopenia" title="Spaceflight osteopenia">spaceflight osteopenia</a>. These effects can be minimized through a regimen of exercise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spp22_15_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-spp22_15-75">[75]</a></sup> Other effects include fluid redistribution, slowing of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_system" title="Cardiovascular system">cardiovascular system</a>, decreased production of <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>, balance disorders, and a weakening of the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>. Lesser symptoms include loss of body mass, nasal congestion, sleep disturbance, and puffiness of the face.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cmaj180_13_1317_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-cmaj180_13_1317-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For long-duration space travel, radiation can pose an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acute_health_hazard" title="Acute health hazard">acute health hazard</a>.\nExposure to high-energy, ionizing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_rays" title="Cosmic rays">cosmic rays</a> can result in fatigue, nausea, vomiting, as well as damage to the immune system and changes to the <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cell</a> count. Over longer durations, symptoms include an increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, plus damage to the <a href="/wiki/Human_eye" title="Human eye">eyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_lung" title="Human lung">lungs</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_gastrointestinal_tract" title="Human gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal tract</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsbri_radiation_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsbri_radiation-77">[77]</a></sup> On a round-trip <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> mission lasting three years, a large fraction of the cells in an astronaut\'s body would be traversed and potentially damaged by high energy nuclei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curtis_and_Letaw_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-curtis_and_Letaw-78">[78]</a></sup> The energy of such particles is significantly diminished by the shielding provided by the walls of a spacecraft and can be further diminished by water containers and other barriers. However, the impact of the cosmic rays upon the shielding produces additional radiation that can affect the crew. Further research is needed to assess the radiation hazards and determine suitable countermeasures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sas4_11_1013_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-sas4_11_1013-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Boundary">Boundary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Boundary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SpaceShipOne_Flight_15P_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg"><img alt="A white rocketship with oddly-shaped wings at rest on a runway." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/SpaceShipOne_Flight_15P_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/220px-SpaceShipOne_Flight_15P_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/SpaceShipOne_Flight_15P_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/330px-SpaceShipOne_Flight_15P_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/SpaceShipOne_Flight_15P_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/440px-SpaceShipOne_Flight_15P_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SpaceShipOne_Flight_15P_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/SpaceShipOne" title="SpaceShipOne">SpaceShipOne</a> completed the first <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">manned</a> <a href="/wiki/Private_spaceflight" title="Private spaceflight">private spaceflight</a> in 2004, reaching an altitude of 100.12&#160;km (62.21&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn_spaceshipone_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn_spaceshipone-80">[80]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>There is no clear boundary between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth\'s atmosphere">Earth\'s atmosphere</a> and space, as the density of the atmosphere gradually decreases as the altitude increases. There are several standard boundary designations, namely:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration_A%C3%A9ronautique_Internationale" title="F&#233;d&#233;ration A&#233;ronautique Internationale">F&#233;d&#233;ration A&#233;ronautique Internationale</a> has established the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n_line" title="K&#225;rm&#225;n line">K&#225;rm&#225;n line</a> at an altitude of 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi) as a working definition for the boundary between aeronautics and astronautics. This is used because at an altitude of about 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi), as <a href="/wiki/Theodore_von_K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n" title="Theodore von K&#225;rm&#225;n">Theodore von K&#225;rm&#225;n</a> calculated, a vehicle would have to travel faster than <a href="/wiki/Orbital_speed" title="Orbital speed">orbital velocity</a> to derive sufficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerodynamic_lift" title="Aerodynamic lift">aerodynamic lift</a> from the atmosphere to support itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO\'Leary200984_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO\'Leary200984-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space_begin_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-space_begin-8">[8]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The United States designates people who travel above an altitude of 50 miles (80&#160;km) as <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">astronauts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWongFergusson201016_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWongFergusson201016-81">[81]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>\'s Space Shuttle used 400,000 feet (76&#160;mi, 122&#160;km) as its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_reentry" title="Atmospheric reentry">re-entry</a> altitude (termed the Entry Interface), which roughly marks the boundary where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_drag" title="Atmospheric drag">atmospheric drag</a> becomes noticeable, thus beginning the process of switching from steering with thrusters to maneuvering with aerodynamic control surfaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-petty20030213_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-petty20030213-82">[82]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>In 2009, scientists reported detailed measurements with a Supra-Thermal Ion Imager (an instrument that measures the direction and speed of ions), which allowed them to establish a boundary at 118&#160;km (73&#160;mi) above Earth. The boundary represents the midpoint of a gradual transition over tens of kilometers from the relatively gentle winds of the Earth\'s atmosphere to the more violent flows of charged particles in space, which can reach speeds well over 268&#160;m/s (600&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thompton20090409_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-thompton20090409-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jgr114_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-jgr114-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_status">Legal status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Legal status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_law" title="Space law">Space law</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SM-3_launch_to_destroy_the_NRO-L_21_satellite.jpg"><img alt="At top, a dark rocket is emitting a bright plume of flame against a blue sky. Underneath, a column of smoke is partly concealing a navy ship." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2100" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/SM-3_launch_to_destroy_the_NRO-L_21_satellite.jpg/170px-SM-3_launch_to_destroy_the_NRO-L_21_satellite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/SM-3_launch_to_destroy_the_NRO-L_21_satellite.jpg/255px-SM-3_launch_to_destroy_the_NRO-L_21_satellite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/SM-3_launch_to_destroy_the_NRO-L_21_satellite.jpg/340px-SM-3_launch_to_destroy_the_NRO-L_21_satellite.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SM-3_launch_to_destroy_the_NRO-L_21_satellite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>2008 launch of the <a href="/wiki/RIM-161_Standard_Missile_3" title="RIM-161 Standard Missile 3">SM-3 missile</a> used to destroy American <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance_satellite" title="Reconnaissance satellite">reconnaissance satellite</a> <a href="/wiki/USA-193" title="USA-193">USA-193</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Outer_Space_Treaty" title="Outer Space Treaty">Outer Space Treaty</a> provides the basic framework for international space law. It covers the legal use of outer space by nation states, and includes in its definition of <i>outer space</i> the Moon and other celestial bodies. The treaty states that outer space is free for all nation states to explore and is not subject to claims of national <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a>. It also prohibits the deployment of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a> in outer space. The treaty was passed by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> in 1963 and signed in 1967 by the USSR, the United States of America and the United Kingdom. As of 2017, 105 state parties have either ratified or acceded to the treaty. An additional 25 states signed the treaty, without ratifying it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unoosa2_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-unoosa2-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unoosa_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-unoosa-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since 1958, outer space has been the subject of multiple United Nations resolutions. Of these, more than 50 have been concerning the international co-operation in the peaceful uses of outer space and preventing an arms race in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garros_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-garros-87">[87]</a></sup> Four additional <a href="/wiki/Space_law" title="Space law">space law</a> treaties have been negotiated and drafted by the UN\'s <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Committee_on_the_Peaceful_Uses_of_Outer_Space" title="United Nations Committee on the Peaceful Uses of Outer Space">Committee on the Peaceful Uses of Outer Space</a>. Still, there remains no legal prohibition against deploying conventional weapons in space, and <a href="/wiki/Anti-satellite_weapon" title="Anti-satellite weapon">anti-satellite weapons</a> have been successfully tested by the US, USSR and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWongFergusson20104_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWongFergusson20104-88">[88]</a></sup> The 1979 <a href="/wiki/Moon_Treaty" title="Moon Treaty">Moon Treaty</a> turned the jurisdiction of all heavenly bodies (including the orbits around such bodies) over to the international community. However, this treaty has not been ratified by any nation that currently practices manned spaceflight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esf20071105_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-esf20071105-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1976, eight equatorial states (<a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Republic of the Congo">Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Zaire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>) met in <a href="/wiki/Bogot%C3%A1" title="Bogot&#225;">Bogot&#225;</a>, Colombia. With their "Declaration of the First Meeting of Equatorial Countries", or "the Bogot&#225; Declaration", they claimed control of the segment of the geosynchronous orbital path corresponding to each country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bogota1976_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-bogota1976-90">[90]</a></sup> These claims are not internationally accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aasl31_2006_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-aasl31_2006-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_orbit">Earth orbit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Earth orbit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_orbit" title="Geocentric orbit">Geocentric orbit</a></div>\n<p>A spacecraft enters orbit when its <a href="/wiki/Centripetal_force" title="Centripetal force">centripetal</a> <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a> due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravity</a> is less than or equal to the <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force" title="Centrifugal force">centrifugal</a> acceleration due to the horizontal component of its velocity. For a <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a>, this velocity is about 7,800&#160;m/s (28,100&#160;km/h; 17,400&#160;mph);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill1999_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-hill1999-92">[92]</a></sup> by contrast, the fastest manned airplane speed ever achieved (excluding speeds achieved by deorbiting spacecraft) was 2,200&#160;m/s (7,900&#160;km/h; 4,900&#160;mph) in 1967 by the <a href="/wiki/North_American_X-15" title="North American X-15">North American X-15</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shiner20071101_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-shiner20071101-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p>To achieve an orbit, a <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> must travel faster than a <a href="/wiki/Sub-orbital_spaceflight" title="Sub-orbital spaceflight">sub-orbital spaceflight</a>. The energy required to reach Earth orbital velocity at an altitude of 600&#160;km (370&#160;mi) is about 36&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megajoule" title="Megajoule">MJ</a>/kg, which is six times the energy needed merely to climb to the corresponding altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dimotakis1999_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-dimotakis1999-94">[94]</a></sup> Spacecraft with a <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">perigee</a> below about 2,000&#160;km (1,200&#160;mi) are subject to drag from the Earth\'s atmosphere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGhosh200047&#8211;48_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGhosh200047&#8211;48-95">[95]</a></sup> which decreases the orbital altitude. The rate of orbital decay depends on the satellite\'s cross-sectional area and mass, as well as variations in the air density of the upper atmosphere. Below about 300&#160;km (190&#160;mi), decay becomes more rapid with lifetimes measured in days. Once a satellite descends to 180&#160;km (110&#160;mi), it has only hours before it vaporizes in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slsa_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-slsa-96">[96]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a> required to pull free of Earth\'s gravitational field altogether and move into interplanetary space is about 11,200&#160;m/s (40,300&#160;km/h; 25,100&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williams2010_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-williams2010-97">[97]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regions">Regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Space is a partial vacuum: its different regions are defined by the various atmospheres and "winds" that dominate within them, and extend to the point at which those winds give way to those beyond. Geospace extends from Earth\'s atmosphere to the outer reaches of Earth\'s magnetic field, whereupon it gives way to the solar wind of interplanetary space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchrijverSiscoe2010363_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchrijverSiscoe2010363-98">[98]</a></sup> Interplanetary space extends to the heliopause, whereupon the solar wind gives way to the winds of the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-universetoday_interplanetaryspace_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-universetoday_interplanetaryspace-99">[99]</a></sup> Interstellar space then continues to the edges of the galaxy, where it fades into the intergalactic void.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl_interstellarspace_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-jpl_interstellarspace-100">[100]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geospace">Geospace</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geospace">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aurora-SpaceShuttle-EO.jpg"><img alt="The lower half is the blue-white planet in low illumination. Nebulous red streamers climb upward from the limb of the disk toward the black sky. The Space Shuttle is visible along the left edge." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1173" data-file-width="1782" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Aurora-SpaceShuttle-EO.jpg/220px-Aurora-SpaceShuttle-EO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Aurora-SpaceShuttle-EO.jpg/330px-Aurora-SpaceShuttle-EO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Aurora-SpaceShuttle-EO.jpg/440px-Aurora-SpaceShuttle-EO.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aurora-SpaceShuttle-EO.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">Aurora australis</a> observed from the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Discovery" title="Space Shuttle Discovery">Discovery</a></i>, on <a href="/wiki/STS-39" title="STS-39">STS-39</a>, May 1991 (orbital altitude: 260 km)</div></div></div>\n<p><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/geospace" title="wikt:geospace">Geospace</a> is the region of outer space near Earth, including the upper atmosphere and <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchrijverSiscoe2010363_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchrijverSiscoe2010363-98">[98]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Van_Allen_radiation_belt" title="Van Allen radiation belt">Van Allen radiation belts</a> lie within the geospace. The outer boundary of geospace is the <a href="/wiki/Magnetopause" title="Magnetopause">magnetopause</a>, which forms an interface between the Earth\'s magnetosphere and the solar wind. The inner boundary is the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geospace_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-geospace-101">[101]</a></sup> The variable space-weather conditions of geospace are affected by the behavior of the Sun and the solar wind; the subject of geospace is interlinked with <a href="/wiki/Heliophysics" title="Heliophysics">heliophysics</a>&#8212;the study of the Sun and its impact on the planets of the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFichtnerLiu2011341&#8211;345_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFichtnerLiu2011341&#8211;345-102">[102]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The day-side magnetopause is compressed by solar-wind pressure&#8212;the subsolar distance from the center of the Earth is typically 10 Earth radii. On the night side, the solar wind stretches the magnetosphere to form a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetotail" title="Magnetotail">magnetotail</a> that sometimes extends out to more than 100&#8211;200 Earth radii.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoskinen201032,_42_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoskinen201032,_42-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonesJr1986_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-HonesJr1986-104">[104]</a></sup> For roughly four days of each month, the lunar surface is shielded from the solar wind as the Moon passes through the magnetotail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMendillo2000275_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMendillo2000275-105">[105]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Geospace is populated by electrically charged particles at very low densities, the motions of which are controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth\'s magnetic field">Earth\'s magnetic field</a>. These plasmas form a medium from which storm-like disturbances powered by the solar wind can drive electrical currents into the Earth\'s upper atmosphere. <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_storm" title="Geomagnetic storm">Geomagnetic storms</a> can disturb two regions of geospace, the radiation belts and the ionosphere. These storms increase fluxes of energetic electrons that can permanently damage satellite electronics, interfering with shortwave radio communication and <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">GPS</a> location and timing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Magnetic storms can also be a hazard to astronauts, even in low Earth orbit. They also create <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">aurorae</a> seen at high latitudes in an oval surrounding the <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_pole" title="Geomagnetic pole">geomagnetic poles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-oecd-107">[107]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although it meets the definition of outer space, the atmospheric density within the first few hundred kilometers above the K&#225;rm&#225;n line is still sufficient to produce significant <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a> on <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slsa_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-slsa-96">[96]</a></sup> This region contains material left over from previous manned and unmanned launches that are a potential hazard to spacecraft. Some of this <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">debris</a> re-enters Earth\'s atmosphere periodically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-portree_loftus1999_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-portree_loftus1999-108">[108]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cislunar_space">Cislunar space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cislunar space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_concept_of_Cislunar_Spacecraft_Configuration.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="918" data-file-width="1241" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Artist%27s_concept_of_Cislunar_Spacecraft_Configuration.jpg/220px-Artist%27s_concept_of_Cislunar_Spacecraft_Configuration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Artist%27s_concept_of_Cislunar_Spacecraft_Configuration.jpg/330px-Artist%27s_concept_of_Cislunar_Spacecraft_Configuration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Artist%27s_concept_of_Cislunar_Spacecraft_Configuration.jpg/440px-Artist%27s_concept_of_Cislunar_Spacecraft_Configuration.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_concept_of_Cislunar_Spacecraft_Configuration.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proposed spacecraft for manned cislunar travel in the <a href="/wiki/2020s_in_spaceflight" title="2020s in spaceflight">2020s</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">Earth\'s gravity</a> keeps the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> in orbit at an <a href="/wiki/Lunar_distance_(astronomy)" title="Lunar distance (astronomy)">average distance</a> of 384,403&#160;km (238,857&#160;mi).
