The region outside <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth\'s atmosphere</a> and extending out to just beyond the <a href="/wiki/Orbit_of_the_Moon" title="Orbit of the Moon">Moon\'s orbit</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian points</a>, is sometimes referred to as <b>cislunar space</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sr2165_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-sr2165-109">[109]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The region of space where Earth\'s gravity remains dominant against gravitational <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbations</a> from the Sun is called the <a href="/wiki/Hill_sphere" title="Hill sphere">Hill sphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yoder1995_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-yoder1995-110">[110]</a></sup> This extends well out into translunar space to a distance of roughly 1% of the mean distance from Earth to the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbieri2006253_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbieri2006253-111">[111]</a></sup> or 1.5&#160;million&#160;km (0.93&#160;million&#160;mi).\n</p><p><b>Deep space</b> has different definitions as to where it starts. It has been defined by the United States government and others as any region beyond cislunar space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDickson201057_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDickson201057-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnited_States536_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnited_States536-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliamson200697_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliamson200697-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-115">[115]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/ITU-R" title="ITU-R">International Telecommunication Union responsible for radio communication</a> (including satellites) defines the beginning of deep space at about 5 times that distance (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7009200000000000000&#9824;</span>2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>6</sup>&#160;km</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interplanetary_space">Interplanetary space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Interplanetary space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">Interplanetary medium</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comet_Hale-Bopp_1995O1.jpg"><img alt="At lower left, a white coma stands out against a black background. Nebulous material streams away to the top and left, slowly fading with distance." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Comet_Hale-Bopp_1995O1.jpg/220px-Comet_Hale-Bopp_1995O1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Comet_Hale-Bopp_1995O1.jpg/330px-Comet_Hale-Bopp_1995O1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Comet_Hale-Bopp_1995O1.jpg/440px-Comet_Hale-Bopp_1995O1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comet_Hale-Bopp_1995O1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The sparse plasma (blue) and dust (white) in the tail of <a href="/wiki/Comet_Hale%E2%80%93Bopp" title="Comet Hale&#8211;Bopp">comet Hale&#8211;Bopp</a> are being shaped by pressure from <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">solar radiation</a> and the solar wind, respectively</div></div></div>\n<p>Interplanetary space is defined by the solar wind, a continuous stream of charged particles emanating from the Sun that creates a very tenuous atmosphere (the <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliosphere</a>) for billions of kilometers into space. This wind has a particle density of 5&#8211;10 <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a>/cm<sup>3</sup> and is moving at a velocity of 350&#8211;400&#160;km/s (780,000&#8211;890,000&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPapagiannis197212&#8211;149_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPapagiannis197212&#8211;149-117">[117]</a></sup> Interplanetary space extends out to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astropause" title="Astropause">heliopause</a> where the influence of the galactic environment starts to dominate over the magnetic field and particle flux from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-universetoday_interplanetaryspace_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-universetoday_interplanetaryspace-99">[99]</a></sup> The distance and strength of the heliopause varies depending on the activity level of the solar wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phillips2009_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-phillips2009-118">[118]</a></sup> The heliopause in turn deflects away low-energy galactic cosmic rays, with this modulation effect peaking during solar maximum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kohler2017_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kohler2017-119">[119]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The volume of interplanetary space is a nearly total vacuum, with a mean free path of about one <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical unit</a> at the orbital distance of the Earth. However, this space is not completely empty, and is sparsely filled with cosmic rays, which include <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionized</a> <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nuclei</a> and various subatomic particles. There is also gas, <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> and dust, small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteor" title="Meteor">meteors</a>, and several dozen types of <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic</a> <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> discovered to date by <a href="/wiki/Rotational_spectroscopy" title="Rotational spectroscopy">microwave spectroscopy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asp2003_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-asp2003-120">[120]</a></sup> A cloud of interplanetary dust is visible at night as a faint band called the <a href="/wiki/Zodiacal_light" title="Zodiacal light">zodiacal light</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leinert_grun_1990_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-leinert_grun_1990-121">[121]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Interplanetary space contains the magnetic field generated by the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPapagiannis197212&#8211;149_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPapagiannis197212&#8211;149-117">[117]</a></sup> There are also magnetospheres generated by planets such as Jupiter, Saturn, Mercury and the Earth that have their own magnetic fields. These are shaped by the influence of the solar wind into the approximation of a teardrop shape, with the long tail extending outward behind the planet. These magnetic fields can trap particles from the solar wind and other sources, creating belts of charged particles such as the Van Allen radiation belts. Planets without magnetic fields, such as Mars, have their atmospheres gradually eroded by the solar wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssr69_3_215_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-ssr69_3_215-122">[122]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interstellar_space">Interstellar space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Interstellar space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">Interstellar medium</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Interstellar space" redirects here. For the album, see <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_Space" title="Interstellar Space">Interstellar Space</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:52706main_hstorion_lg.jpg"><img alt="Patchy orange and blue nebulosity against a black background, with a curved orange arc wrapping around a star at the center." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="895" data-file-width="1074" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/52706main_hstorion_lg.jpg/220px-52706main_hstorion_lg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/52706main_hstorion_lg.jpg/330px-52706main_hstorion_lg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/52706main_hstorion_lg.jpg/440px-52706main_hstorion_lg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:52706main_hstorion_lg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_shock" title="Bow shock">Bow shock</a> formed by the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> of the young star <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=LL_Orionis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="LL Orionis (page does not exist)">LL Orionis</a> (center) as it collides with the <a href="/wiki/Orion_Nebula" title="Orion Nebula">Orion Nebula</a> flow</div></div></div>\n<p>Interstellar space is the physical space within a galaxy beyond the influence each star has upon the encompassed plasma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl_interstellarspace_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-jpl_interstellarspace-100">[100]</a></sup> The contents of interstellar space are called the interstellar medium. Approximately 70% of the mass of the interstellar medium consists of lone hydrogen atoms; most of the remainder consists of helium atoms. This is enriched with trace amounts of heavier atoms formed through <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>. These atoms are ejected into the interstellar medium by <a href="/wiki/Stellar_wind" title="Stellar wind">stellar winds</a> or when evolved stars begin to shed their outer envelopes such as during the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_nebula" title="Planetary nebula">planetary nebula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferri&#232;re2001_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferri&#232;re2001-123">[123]</a></sup> The cataclysmic explosion of a <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> generates an expanding <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock wave</a> consisting of ejected materials that further enrich the medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witt2001_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-witt2001-124">[124]</a></sup> The density of matter in the interstellar medium can vary considerably: the average is around 10<sup>6</sup> particles per m<sup>3</sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boulares1990_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boulares1990-125">[125]</a></sup> but cold <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_clouds" title="Molecular clouds">molecular clouds</a> can hold 10<sup>8</sup>&#8211;10<sup>12</sup> per m<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrialnik2000195&#8211;196_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrialnik2000195&#8211;196-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferri&#232;re2001_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ferri&#232;re2001-123">[123]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/List_of_interstellar_and_circumstellar_molecules" title="List of interstellar and circumstellar molecules">number of molecules</a> exist in interstellar space, as can tiny 0.1&#160;<a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">&#956;m</a> dust particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERauchfuss200872&#8211;81_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERauchfuss200872&#8211;81-126">[126]</a></sup> The tally of molecules discovered through <a href="/wiki/Radio_astronomy" title="Radio astronomy">radio astronomy</a> is steadily increasing at the rate of about four new species per year. Large regions of higher density matter known as <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular clouds</a> allow chemical reactions to occur, including the formation of organic polyatomic species. Much of this chemistry is driven by collisions. Energetic cosmic rays penetrate the cold, dense clouds and ionize hydrogen and helium, resulting, for example, in the <a href="/wiki/Trihydrogen_cation" title="Trihydrogen cation">trihydrogen cation</a>. An ionized helium atom can then split relatively abundant <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> to produce ionized carbon, which in turn can lead to organic chemical reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS103_33_12232_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS103_33_12232-127">[127]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The local interstellar medium is a region of space within 100&#160;<a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsecs</a> (pc) of the Sun, which is of interest both for its proximity and for its interaction with the Solar System. This volume nearly coincides with a region of space known as the <a href="/wiki/Local_Bubble" title="Local Bubble">Local Bubble</a>, which is characterized by a lack of dense, cold clouds. It forms a cavity in the <a href="/wiki/Orion_Arm" title="Orion Arm">Orion Arm</a> of the Milky Way galaxy, with dense molecular clouds lying along the borders, such as those in the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellations</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ophiuchus" title="Ophiuchus">Ophiuchus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taurus_(constellation)" title="Taurus (constellation)">Taurus</a>. (The actual distance to the border of this cavity varies from 60 to 250&#160;pc or more.) This volume contains about 10<sup>4</sup>&#8211;10<sup>5</sup> stars and the local interstellar gas counterbalances the <a href="/wiki/Stellar-wind_bubble" title="Stellar-wind bubble">astrospheres</a> that surround these stars, with the volume of each sphere varying depending on the local density of the interstellar medium. The Local Bubble contains dozens of warm interstellar clouds with temperatures of up to 7,000&#160;K and radii of 0.5&#8211;5&#160;pc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redfield2006_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-redfield2006-128">[128]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When stars are moving at sufficiently high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peculiar_velocities" title="Peculiar velocities">peculiar velocities</a>, their astrospheres can generate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_shock" title="Bow shock">bow shocks</a> as they collide with the interstellar medium. For decades it was assumed that the Sun had a bow shock. In 2012, data from <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_Boundary_Explorer" title="Interstellar Boundary Explorer">Interstellar Boundary Explorer (IBEX)</a> and NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a> probes showed that the Sun\'s bow shock does not exist. Instead, these authors argue that a <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">subsonic</a> bow wave defines the transition from the solar wind flow to the interstellar medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bow_science_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-bow_science-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bow_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-bow-130">[130]</a></sup> A bow shock is the third boundary of an astrosphere after the <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere#Termination_shock" title="Heliosphere">termination shock</a> and the astropause (called the heliopause in the Solar System).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bow_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-bow-130">[130]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intergalactic_space">Intergalactic space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Intergalactic space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LH_95.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3877" data-file-width="3622" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/LH_95.jpg/220px-LH_95.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/LH_95.jpg/330px-LH_95.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/LH_95.jpg/440px-LH_95.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LH_95.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/LH_95" title="LH 95">A</a> <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a>-forming region in the <a href="/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Large Magellanic Cloud">Large Magellanic Cloud</a>, perhaps the closest Galaxy to Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Intracluster_medium" title="Intracluster medium">Intracluster medium</a></div>\n<p>Intergalactic space is the physical space between galaxies. Studies of the large scale distribution of galaxies show that the Universe has a foam-like structure, with <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_groups_and_clusters" title="Galaxy groups and clusters">clusters and groups of galaxies</a> lying along filaments that occupy about a tenth of the total space. The remainder forms huge voids that are mostly empty of galaxies. Typically, a <a href="/wiki/Void_(astronomy)" title="Void (astronomy)">void</a> spans a distance of (10&#8211;40) <i>h</i><sup>&#8722;1</sup> Mpc, where <i>h</i> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_constant" title="Hubble constant">Hubble constant</a> in units of <span class="nowrap">100 km s<sup>&#8722;1</sup> Mpc<sup>&#8722;1</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWszolek201367_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWszolek201367-131">[131]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Surrounding and stretching between galaxies, there is a <a href="/wiki/Rarefaction" title="Rarefaction">rarefied</a> plasma<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jafelice_opher1992_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-jafelice_opher1992-132">[132]</a></sup> that is organized in a <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_filament" title="Galaxy filament">galactic filamentary</a> structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wadsley2002_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-wadsley2002-133">[133]</a></sup> This material is called the intergalactic medium (IGM). The density of the IGM is 5&#8211;200 times the average density of the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj_714_1715_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj_714_1715-134">[134]</a></sup> It consists mostly of ionized hydrogen; i.e. a plasma consisting of equal numbers of electrons and protons. As gas falls into the intergalactic medium from the voids, it heats up to temperatures of 10<sup>5</sup>&#160;K to 10<sup>7</sup>&#160;K,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baas41_908_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-baas41_908-2">[2]</a></sup> which is high enough so that collisions between atoms have enough energy to cause the bound electrons to escape from the hydrogen nuclei; this is why the IGM is ionized. At these temperatures, it is called the <a href="/wiki/Warm%E2%80%93hot_intergalactic_medium" title="Warm&#8211;hot intergalactic medium">warm&#8211;hot intergalactic medium</a> (WHIM). (Although the plasma is very hot by terrestrial standards, 10<sup>5</sup> K is often called "warm" in astrophysics.) Computer simulations and observations indicate that up to half of the atomic matter in the Universe might exist in this warm&#8211;hot, rarefied state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj_714_1715_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-apj_714_1715-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssr134_1_141_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-ssr134_1_141-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apjs_182_378_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-apjs_182_378-136">[136]</a></sup> When gas falls from the filamentary structures of the WHIM into the galaxy clusters at the intersections of the cosmic filaments, it can heat up even more, reaching temperatures of 10<sup>8</sup>&#160;K and above in the so-called intracluster medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj546_100_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj546_100-137">[137]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration_and_applications">Exploration and applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Exploration and applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">Space exploration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Space_colonization" title="Space colonization">Space colonization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Space_manufacturing" title="Space manufacturing">Space manufacturing</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:As08-16-2593.jpg"><img alt="An blue-white disk against a black background. Brown areas of ground are visible in some areas through openings in the swirling white clouds. The lower left of the disk is in partial shadow." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1781" data-file-width="2015" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/As08-16-2593.jpg/220px-As08-16-2593.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/As08-16-2593.jpg/330px-As08-16-2593.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/As08-16-2593.jpg/440px-As08-16-2593.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:As08-16-2593.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first image taken of the entire Earth by astronauts was shot during the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a> mission</div></div></div>\n<p>For the majority of human history, space was explored by observations made from the Earth\'s surface&#8212;initially with the unaided eye and then with the telescope. Prior to the advent of reliable rocket technology, the closest that humans had come to reaching outer space was through the use of balloon flights. In 1935, the U.S. <i><a href="/wiki/Explorer_II" title="Explorer II">Explorer II</a></i> manned balloon flight had reached an altitude of 22&#160;km (14&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssr13_2_199_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-ssr13_2_199-138">[138]</a></sup> This was greatly exceeded in 1942 when the third launch of the German <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">A-4 rocket</a> climbed to an altitude of about 80&#160;km (50&#160;mi). In 1957, the unmanned satellite <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik 1</a> was launched by a Russian <a href="/wiki/R-7_Semyorka" title="R-7 Semyorka">R-7 rocket</a>, achieving Earth orbit at an altitude of 215&#8211;939 kilometres (134&#8211;583&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO\'Leary2009209&#8211;224_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO\'Leary2009209&#8211;224-139">[139]</a></sup> This was followed by the first human spaceflight in 1961, when <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a> was sent into orbit on <a href="/wiki/Vostok_1" title="Vostok 1">Vostok 1</a>. The first humans to escape low-Earth orbit were <a href="/wiki/Frank_Borman" title="Frank Borman">Frank Borman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jim_Lovell" title="Jim Lovell">Jim Lovell</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Anders" title="William Anders">William Anders</a> in 1968 on board the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a>, which achieved lunar orbit<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison200260&#8211;63_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison200260&#8211;63-140">[140]</a></sup> and reached a maximum distance of 377,349&#160;km (234,474&#160;mi) from the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff2001_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff2001-141">[141]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first spacecraft to reach escape velocity was the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Luna_1" title="Luna 1">Luna 1</a>, which performed a fly-by of the Moon in 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardestyEismanKrushchev200889&#8211;90_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardestyEismanKrushchev200889&#8211;90-142">[142]</a></sup> In 1961, <a href="/wiki/Venera_1" title="Venera 1">Venera 1</a> became the first planetary probe. It revealed the presence of the solar wind and performed the first fly-by of <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, although contact was lost before reaching Venus. The first successful planetary mission was the 1962 fly-by of Venus by <a href="/wiki/Mariner_2" title="Mariner 2">Mariner 2</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins200786_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins200786-143">[143]</a></sup> The first fly-by of Mars was by <a href="/wiki/Mariner_4" title="Mariner 4">Mariner 4</a> in 1964. Since that time, unmanned spacecraft have successfully examined each of the Solar System\'s planets, as well their moons and many <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planets</a> and comets. They remain a fundamental tool for the exploration of outer space, as well as observation of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris20087,_68&#8211;69_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris20087,_68&#8211;69-144">[144]</a></sup> In August 2012, <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> became the first man-made object to leave the Solar System and enter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_space" title="Interstellar space">interstellar space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mike_wall_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-mike_wall-145">[145]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The absence of air makes outer space an ideal location for astronomy at all wavelengths of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">electromagnetic spectrum</a>. This is evidenced by the spectacular pictures sent back by the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a>, allowing light from more than 13&#160;billion years ago&#8212;almost to the time of the Big Bang&#8212;to be observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubblesite_cosmicdawn_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-hubblesite_cosmicdawn-146">[146]</a></sup> However, not every location in space is ideal for a telescope. The <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_dust_cloud" title="Interplanetary dust cloud">interplanetary zodiacal dust</a> emits a diffuse near-infrared radiation that can mask the emission of faint sources such as extrasolar planets. Moving an <a href="/wiki/Infrared_telescope" title="Infrared telescope">infrared telescope</a> out past the dust increases its effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa105_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa105-147">[147]</a></sup> Likewise, a site like the <a href="/wiki/Daedalus_(crater)" title="Daedalus (crater)">Daedalus crater</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon" title="Far side of the Moon">far side of the Moon</a> could shield a <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">radio telescope</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_interference" title="Electromagnetic interference">radio frequency interference</a> that hampers Earth-based observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maccone2001_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-maccone2001-148">[148]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unmanned spacecraft in Earth orbit are an essential technology of modern civilization. They allow direct monitoring of <a href="/wiki/Weather_satellite" title="Weather satellite">weather conditions</a>, relay <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">long-range communications</a> like television, provide a means of <a href="/wiki/Satellite_navigation" title="Satellite navigation">precise navigation</a>, and allow <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remote sensing</a> of the Earth. The latter role serves a wide variety of purposes, including tracking soil moisture for agriculture, prediction of water outflow from seasonal snow packs, detection of diseases in plants and trees, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spy_satellite" title="Spy satellite">surveillance</a> of military activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERazani201297&#8211;99_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERazani201297&#8211;99-149">[149]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The deep vacuum of space could make it an attractive environment for certain industrial processes, such as those requiring ultraclean surfaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chapman1991_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-chapman1991-150">[150]</a></sup> However, like <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_mining" title="Asteroid mining">asteroid mining</a>, <a href="/wiki/Space_manufacturing" title="Space manufacturing">space manufacturing</a> requires significant investment with little prospect of immediate return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IJA10_307_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-IJA10_307-151">[151]</a></sup> An important factor in the total expense is the high cost of placing mass into Earth orbit: $7,000&#8211;25,000 per kg in inflation-adjusted dollars, according to a 2006 estimate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jsr43_3_696_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-jsr43_3_696-152">[152]</a></sup> Proposed concepts for addressing this issue include <a href="/wiki/Non-rocket_spacelaunch" title="Non-rocket spacelaunch">non-rocket spacelaunch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Momentum_exchange_tether" title="Momentum exchange tether">momentum exchange tethers</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_elevators" title="Space elevators">space elevators</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBolonkin2010xv_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBolonkin2010xv-153">[153]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Interstellar_travel" title="Interstellar travel">Interstellar travel</a> for a human crew remains at present only a theoretical possibility. The distances to the nearest stars will require new technological developments and the ability to safely sustain crews for journeys lasting several decades. For example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daedalus_Project" title="Daedalus Project">Daedalus Project</a> study, which proposed a spacecraft powered by the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">fusion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">Deuterium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">He<sup>3</sup></a>, would require 36 years to reach the nearby <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri" title="Alpha Centauri">Alpha Centauri</a> system. Other proposed interstellar propulsion systems include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_sail" title="Light sail">light sails</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bussard_ramjet" title="Bussard ramjet">ramjets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Beam-powered_propulsion" title="Beam-powered propulsion">beam-powered propulsion</a>. More advanced propulsion systems could use <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a> as a fuel, potentially reaching <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_speed" title="Relativistic speed">relativistic velocities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crawford1990_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crawford1990-154">[154]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="28" data-file-width="32"
